	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" >Influenza</h1>
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		<div id="contentSub2"></div>
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="Influenza#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="Influenza#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Infectious disease, "the flu"</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Influenza (disambiguation)">Influenza (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haemophilus_influenzae" title="Haemophilus influenzae">Haemophilus influenzae</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Flu" and "Grippe" redirect here. For other uses, see <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flu_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Flu (disambiguation)">Flu (disambiguation)</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grippe_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Grippe (disambiguation)">Grippe (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;background:#ccc">Influenza</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Other title="Rhinorrhea">runny nose</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sore_throat" title="Sore throat">sore throat</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Myalgia" title="Myalgia">muscle and joint pain</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">headache</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cough" title="Cough">coughing</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fatigue_(medical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fatigue (medical)">feeling tired</a><sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Usual onset</th><td>One to four days after exposure<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Duration</th><td>~1 week<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Causes</th><td><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_viruses" class="mw-redirect" title="Influenza viruses">Influenza viruses</a><sup id="cite_ref-Harr2012_2-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Harr2012-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Prevention</th><td><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hand_washing" title="Hand washing">Hand washing</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_vaccine" title="Influenza vaccine">influenza vaccine</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surgical_mask" title="Surgical mask">surgical masks</a><sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-3" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jeff2011_3-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Jeff2011-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Medication</th><td><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuraminidase_inhibitor" title="Neuraminidase inhibitor">Neuraminidase inhibitors</a> such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oseltamivir" title="Oseltamivir">oseltamivir</a><sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-4" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Frequency</th><td>3–5 million severe cases per year<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-5" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Deaths</th><td>Up to 650,000 respiratory deaths per year<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-6" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WHO2017_4-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2017-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Influenza</b>, commonly known as "<b>the flu</b>", is an <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infectious_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Infectious disease">infectious disease</a> caused by an <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_virus" class="mw-redirect" title="Influenza virus">influenza virus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-7" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symptoms" class="mw-redirect" title="Symptoms">Symptoms</a> can range from mild to severe and commonly include: high <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">fever</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhinorrhea" title="Rhinorrhea">runny nose</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sore_throat" title="Sore throat">sore throat</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Myalgia" title="Myalgia">muscle and joint pain</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">headache</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cough" title="Cough">coughing</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fatigue_(medical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fatigue (medical)">feeling tired</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CDC2014Key_5-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-CDC2014Key-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> These symptoms typically begin two days after exposure to the virus and most last less than a week, although the coughing may last for more than two weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-8" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Einav2020_6-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Einav2020-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In children, there may be <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, but these are not common in adults.<sup id="cite_ref-Dub2011_7-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Dub2011-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Diarrhea and vomiting occur more commonly in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gastroenteritis" title="Gastroenteritis">gastroenteritis</a>, which is an unrelated disease sometimes referred to as "stomach flu" or the "24-hour flu".<sup id="cite_ref-Dub2011_7-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Dub2011-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Complications of influenza may include <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viral_pneumonia" title="Viral pneumonia">viral pneumonia</a>, secondary <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacterial_pneumonia" title="Bacterial pneumonia">bacterial pneumonia</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sinus_infection" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinus infection">sinus infections</a>, and worsening of previous health problems such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a> or <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Harr2012_2-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Harr2012-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CDC2014Key_5-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-CDC2014Key-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Three of the four types of influenza viruses affect humans: Type A, Type B, and Type C.<sup id="cite_ref-Harr2012_2-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Harr2012-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CDC2017Types_8-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-CDC2017Types-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Type D has not been known to infect humans, but is believed to have the potential to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-CDC2017Types_8-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-CDC2017Types-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-InfD2017_9-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-InfD2017-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Usually, the virus is <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Airborne_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Airborne disease">spread through the air</a> from coughs or sneezes.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-9" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> This is believed to occur mostly over relatively short distances.<sup id="cite_ref-Brankston2007_10-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Brankston2007-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It can also be spread by touching surfaces contaminated by the virus and then touching the eyes, nose, or mouth.<sup id="cite_ref-CDC2014Key_5-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-CDC2014Key-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brankston2007_10-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Brankston2007-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> A person may be infectious to others both before and during the time they are showing symptoms.<sup id="cite_ref-CDC2014Key_5-3" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-CDC2014Key-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The infection may be confirmed by testing the throat, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sputum" title="Sputum">sputum</a>, or nose for the virus.<sup id="cite_ref-Harr2012_2-3" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Harr2012-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> A number of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rapid_influenza_diagnostic_test" title="Rapid influenza diagnostic test">rapid tests</a> are available; however, people may still have the infection even if the results are negative.<sup id="cite_ref-Harr2012_2-4" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Harr2012-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> A type of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Polymerase chain reaction">polymerase chain reaction</a> that detects the virus's <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> is more accurate.<sup id="cite_ref-Harr2012_2-5" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Harr2012-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Frequent <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hand_washing" title="Hand washing">hand washing</a> reduces the risk of viral spread, as does wearing a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surgical_mask" title="Surgical mask">surgical mask</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jeff2011_3-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Jeff2011-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="Updated version https&#58;//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/33215698 (December 2020)">needs update</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Yearly <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_vaccine" title="Influenza vaccine">vaccinations against influenza</a> are recommended by the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) for those at high risk,<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-10" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> and by the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> (CDC) for those six months of age and older.<sup id="cite_ref-CDC2019-20_12-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-CDC2019-20-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The vaccine is typically effective against three or four types of influenza and is usually well tolerated.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-11" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> A vaccine made for one year may not be useful in the following year, since the virus evolves rapidly.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-12" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiviral_drug" title="Antiviral drug">Antiviral medications</a> such as the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuraminidase_inhibitor" title="Neuraminidase inhibitor">neuraminidase inhibitor</a> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oseltamivir" title="Oseltamivir">oseltamivir</a>, among others, have been used to treat influenza.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-13" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The benefit of antiviral medications in those who are otherwise healthy do not appear to be greater than their risks.<sup id="cite_ref-Mich2013_13-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Mich2013-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> No benefit has been found in those with other health problems.<sup id="cite_ref-Mich2013_13-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Mich2013-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ebe2013_14-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Ebe2013-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Influenza spreads around the world in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flu_season" title="Flu season">yearly outbreaks</a>, resulting in about three to five million cases of severe illness and about 290,000 to 650,000 deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-14" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WHO2017_4-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2017-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> About 20% of unvaccinated children and 10% of unvaccinated adults are infected each year, according to a 2018 studythr <sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> In the northern and southern <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_latitudes" title="Middle latitudes">parts</a> of the world, outbreaks occur mainly in the winter, while around the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>, outbreaks may occur at any time of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-15" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Death occurs mostly in high risk groups—the young, the old, and those with other health problems.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-16" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Larger outbreaks known as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemics</a> are less frequent.<sup id="cite_ref-Harr2012_2-6" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Harr2012-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In the 20th century, four major <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_pandemic" title="Influenza pandemic">influenza pandemics</a> occurred: the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spanish_flu" title="Spanish flu">Spanish flu</a> in 1918 (17–100<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million deaths), the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1957%E2%80%931958_influenza_pandemic" title="1957–1958 influenza pandemic">Asian flu</a> in 1957 (1–4 million deaths), the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hong_Kong_flu" title="Hong Kong flu">Hong Kong flu</a> in 1968 (1–4 million deaths) and the  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1977_Russian_flu" title="1977 Russian flu">Russian flu</a> in 1977 (700,000 deaths).<sup id="cite_ref-:1_16-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-:1-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_17-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-:0-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Spreeuwenberg1_18-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Spreeuwenberg1-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TenThings_19-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-TenThings-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> declared an outbreak of a new type of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_A_virus_subtype_H1N1" title="Influenza A virus subtype H1N1">influenza A/H1N1</a> to be a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2009_flu_pandemic" class="mw-redirect" title="2009 flu pandemic">pandemic in June 2009</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Influenza may also affect other animals, including pigs, horses, and birds.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; text-align:center; width:40%;">
<caption>Most sensitive symptoms for diagnosing influenza<sup id="cite_ref-pmid15728170_23-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-pmid15728170-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Symptom:</th>
<th><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sensitivity_(tests)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sensitivity (tests)">Sensitivity</a></th>
<th><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Specificity_(tests)" class="mw-redirect" title="Specificity (tests)">Specificity</a>
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th>Fever
</th>
<td>68–86%</td>
<td>25–73%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>Cough
</th>
<td>84–98%</td>
<td>7–29%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>Nasal congestion
</th>
<td>68–91%</td>
<td>19–41%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" style="text-align:left;">
<p><small>
</small></p><small>
<ul><li>All three findings, especially fever, were less sensitive in people over 60 years of age.</li></ul>
</small><p><small></small>
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Symptoms_of_influenza.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Symptoms_of_influenza.svg/300px-Symptoms_of_influenza.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="263" class="thumbimage" srcset="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Symptoms_of_influenza.svg/450px-Symptoms_of_influenza.svg.png 1.5x, https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Symptoms_of_influenza.svg/600px-Symptoms_of_influenza.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1050" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Symptoms_of_influenza.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Symptoms of influenza,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> with fever and cough the most common symptoms.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid15728170_23-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-pmid15728170-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Approximately 33% of people with influenza are asymptomatic.<sup id="cite_ref-AmericanJournalEpidemiology2008_26-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-AmericanJournalEpidemiology2008-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Symptoms of influenza can start quite suddenly three to four days after infection.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Usually the first symptoms are <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chills" title="Chills">chills</a> and body aches, with <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">fever</a> also common early in the infection, with body temperatures ranging from 38 to 39<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>°C (approximately 100 to 103<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>°F).<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Many people are so ill that they are confined to bed for several days, with aches and pains throughout their bodies, which are worse in their backs and legs.<sup id="cite_ref-Merck_30-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Merck-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symptoms_of_influenza">Symptoms of influenza</span></h3>
<ul><li>Fever and chills</li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cough" title="Cough">Cough</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nasal_congestion" title="Nasal congestion">Nasal congestion</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhinorrhea" title="Rhinorrhea">Runny nose</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sore_throat" title="Sore throat">Sore throat</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hoarseness" class="mw-redirect" title="Hoarseness">Hoarseness</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earache" class="mw-redirect" title="Earache">Earache</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Myalgia" title="Myalgia">Muscle pains</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fatigue_(medical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fatigue (medical)">Fatigue</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">Headache</a></li>
<li>Irritated, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tears" title="Tears">watering eyes</a></li>
<li>Reddened eyes, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flushing_(physiology)" title="Flushing (physiology)">skin (especially face)</a>, mouth, throat and nose</li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petechia" title="Petechia">Petechial rash</a><sup id="cite_ref-pmid10476766_31-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-pmid10476766-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In children, gastrointestinal symptoms such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abdominal_pain" title="Abdominal pain">abdominal pain</a><sup id="cite_ref-Richards_32-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Richards-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> (may be severe in children with influenza B)<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>It can be difficult to distinguish between the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_cold" title="Common cold">common cold</a> and influenza in the early stages of these infections.<sup id="cite_ref-Eccles_35-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Eccles-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Influenza symptoms are a mixture of symptoms of common cold and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, body ache, headache, and fatigue. Diarrhea is not usually a symptom of influenza in adults,<sup id="cite_ref-pmid15728170_23-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-pmid15728170-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> although it has been seen in some human cases of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H5N1" class="mw-redirect" title="H5N1">H5N1</a> "bird flu"<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and can be a symptom in children.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards_32-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Richards-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The symptoms most reliably seen in influenza are shown in the adjacent table.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid15728170_23-3" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-pmid15728170-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The specific combination of fever and cough has been found to be the best predictor; diagnostic accuracy increases with a body temperature above 38&#160;°C (100.4&#160;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-pmid11088084_37-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-pmid11088084-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Two <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decision_analysis" title="Decision analysis">decision analysis</a> studies<sup id="cite_ref-pmif12361816_38-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-pmif12361816-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pimd12965940_39-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-pimd12965940-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> suggest that during local outbreaks of influenza, the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prevalence" title="Prevalence">prevalence</a> will be over 70%.<sup id="cite_ref-pimd12965940_39-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-pimd12965940-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Even in the absence of a local outbreak, diagnosis may be justified in the elderly during the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flu_season" title="Flu season">influenza season</a> as long as the prevalence is over 15%.<sup id="cite_ref-pimd12965940_39-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-pimd12965940-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The United States <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> (CDC) maintains an up-to-date summary of available laboratory tests.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> According to the CDC, rapid diagnostic tests have a sensitivity of 50–75% and specificity of 90–95% when compared with <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viral_culture" title="Viral culture">viral culture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-rapidlab_41-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-rapidlab-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Occasionally, influenza can cause severe illness including primary <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viral_pneumonia" title="Viral pneumonia">viral pneumonia</a> or secondary <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacterial_pneumonia" title="Bacterial pneumonia">bacterial pneumonia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-New-England-Journal-Medicine-Hospitalized-H1N1-Patients-2009_42-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-New-England-Journal-Medicine-Hospitalized-H1N1-Patients-2009-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WHO-press-conference-Dr-Nikki-Shindo-12-Nov-2009_43-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO-press-conference-Dr-Nikki-Shindo-12-Nov-2009-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The obvious symptom is trouble breathing. In addition, if a child (or presumably an adult) seems to be getting better and then relapses with a high fever, that is a danger sign since this relapse can be bacterial pneumonia.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimesDeniseGradySept2009_44-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-NYTimesDeniseGradySept2009-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sometimes, influenza may have abnormal presentations, like confusion in the elderly and a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">sepsis</a>-like syndrome in the young.<sup id="cite_ref-cdc.gov_45-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-cdc.gov-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergency_warning_signs">Emergency warning signs</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shortness_of_breath" title="Shortness of breath">Shortness of breath</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chest_pain" title="Chest pain">Chest pain</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dizziness" title="Dizziness">Dizziness</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Confusion" title="Confusion">Confusion</a></li>
<li>Extreme <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a></li>
<li>Flu symptoms that improve but then relapse with a high fever and severe cough (can be bacterial pneumonia)</li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyanosis" title="Cyanosis">Cyanosis</a></li>
<li>High fever and a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rash" title="Rash">rash</a>.</li>
<li>Inability to drink fluids</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_of_dehydration">Signs of dehydration</span></h4>
<ul><li>(In infants) far fewer wet diapers than usual<sup id="cite_ref-CDC_Sick_46-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-CDC_Sick-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Cannot keep down fluids</li>
<li>(In infants) no tears when crying</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Virology">Virology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_virus">Types of virus</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:3D_Influenza_virus.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/3D_Influenza_virus.png/310px-3D_Influenza_virus.png" decoding="async" width="310" height="230" class="thumbimage" srcset="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3b/3D_Influenza_virus.png 1.5x" data-file-width="356" data-file-height="264" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:3D_Influenza_virus.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of the influenza <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/virion" class="extiw" title="wikt:virion">virion</a>. The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemagglutinin" title="Hemagglutinin">hemagglutinin</a> (HA) and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuraminidase" title="Neuraminidase">neuraminidase</a> (NA) proteins are shown on the surface of the particle. The viral RNAs that make up the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> are shown as red coils inside the particle and bound to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_virus_nucleoprotein" title="Influenza virus nucleoprotein">ribonucleoproteins</a> (RNP).</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virus_classification" title="Virus classification">virus classification</a>, influenza viruses are <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Negative-strand_RNA_virus" title="Negative-strand RNA virus">negative sense</a> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA_viruses" class="mw-redirect" title="RNA viruses">RNA viruses</a> that make up four of the seven <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genera" class="mw-redirect" title="Genera">genera</a> of the family <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orthomyxoviridae" title="Orthomyxoviridae">Orthomyxoviridae</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-Kawaoka_47-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Kawaoka-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenzavirus_A" class="mw-redirect" title="Influenzavirus A">Influenzavirus A</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenzavirus_B" class="mw-redirect" title="Influenzavirus B">Influenzavirus B</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenzavirus_C" class="mw-redirect" title="Influenzavirus C">Influenzavirus C</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenzavirus_D" class="mw-redirect" title="Influenzavirus D">Influenzavirus D</a></li></ul>
<p>These viruses are only distantly related to the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_parainfluenza_viruses" title="Human parainfluenza viruses">human parainfluenza viruses</a>, which are RNA viruses belonging to the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paramyxoviridae" title="Paramyxoviridae">paramyxovirus</a> family that are a common cause of respiratory infections in children such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Croup" title="Croup">croup</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> but can also cause a disease similar to influenza in adults.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The fourth family of influenza viruses – <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenzavirus_D" class="mw-redirect" title="Influenzavirus D">influenza D</a> – was identified in 2016.The type species for this family is Influenza D virus, which was first isolated in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-InfD2017_9-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-InfD2017-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Influenzavirus_A">Influenzavirus A</span></h4>
<p>This genus has one species, influenza A virus. Wild aquatic birds are the natural hosts for a large variety of influenza A.<sup id="cite_ref-sobrino6_50-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-sobrino6-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Occasionally, viruses are transmitted to other species and may then cause devastating outbreaks in domestic poultry or give rise to human influenza <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sobrino6_50-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-sobrino6-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The influenza A virus can be subdivided into different <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serovar" class="mw-redirect" title="Serovar">serotypes</a> based on the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibody</a> response to these viruses.<sup id="cite_ref-hay_51-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-hay-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The serotypes that have been confirmed in humans are:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H1N1" class="mw-redirect" title="H1N1">H1N1</a>, which caused <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spanish_flu" title="Spanish flu">Spanish flu</a> in 1918, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1977_Russian_flu" title="1977 Russian flu">Russian flu</a> in 1977, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2009_flu_pandemic" class="mw-redirect" title="2009 flu pandemic">Swine Flu</a> in 2009</li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H2N2" class="mw-redirect" title="H2N2">H2N2</a>, which caused <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asian_Flu" class="mw-redirect" title="Asian Flu">Asian Flu</a> in 1957</li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H3N2" class="mw-redirect" title="H3N2">H3N2</a>, which caused <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hong_Kong_Flu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hong Kong Flu">Hong Kong Flu</a> in 1968</li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H5N1" class="mw-redirect" title="H5N1">H5N1</a>, which caused <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_A_virus_subtype_H5N1" title="Influenza A virus subtype H5N1">Bird Flu</a> in 2004<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H7N7" class="mw-redirect" title="H7N7">H7N7</a>, which has unusual <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zoonotic" class="mw-redirect" title="Zoonotic">zoonotic</a> potential<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H1N2" class="mw-redirect" title="H1N2">H1N2</a>, endemic in humans, pigs and birds</li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H9N2" class="mw-redirect" title="H9N2">H9N2</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H7N2" class="mw-redirect" title="H7N2">H7N2</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H7N3" class="mw-redirect" title="H7N3">H7N3</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H10N7" class="mw-redirect" title="H10N7">H10N7</a></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H7N9" class="mw-redirect" title="H7N9">H7N9</a>, rated in 2018 as having the greatest pandemic potential among the Type A subtypes<sup id="cite_ref-H7N9_virus_55-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-H7N9_virus-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_A_virus_subtype_H6N1" title="Influenza A virus subtype H6N1">H6N1</a>, which only infected one person, who recovered<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Influenzavirus_B">Influenzavirus B</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Influenza_nomenclature.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Influenza_nomenclature.svg/310px-Influenza_nomenclature.svg.png" decoding="async" width="310" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Influenza_nomenclature.svg/465px-Influenza_nomenclature.svg.png 1.5x, https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Influenza_nomenclature.svg/620px-Influenza_nomenclature.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="590" data-file-height="380" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Influenza_nomenclature.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Influenza virus <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Committee_on_Taxonomy_of_Viruses" title="International Committee on Taxonomy of Viruses">nomenclature</a> (for a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fujian_flu" title="Fujian flu">Fujian flu</a> virus)</div></div></div>
<p>This genus has one species, influenza B virus. Influenza B almost exclusively infects humans<sup id="cite_ref-hay_51-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-hay-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> and is less common than influenza A. The only other animals known to be susceptible to influenza B infection are  <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferret" title="Ferret">ferrets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> This type of influenza mutates at a rate 2–3 times slower than type A<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> and consequently is less genetically diverse, with only one influenza B serotype.<sup id="cite_ref-hay_51-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-hay-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> As a result of this lack of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigenic</a> diversity, a degree of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a> to influenza B is usually acquired at an early age. However, influenza B mutates enough that lasting immunity is not possible.<sup id="cite_ref-webster_60-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-webster-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> This reduced rate of antigenic change, combined with its limited host range (inhibiting cross species <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antigenic_shift" title="Antigenic shift">antigenic shift</a>), ensures that pandemics of influenza B do not occur.<sup id="cite_ref-Zambon_61-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Zambon-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Influenzavirus_C">Influenzavirus C</span></h4>
<p>This genus has one species, influenza C virus, which infects humans, dogs and pigs, sometimes causing both severe illness and local epidemics.<sup id="cite_ref-Matsuzaki_62-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Matsuzaki-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Taubenberger2008_63-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Taubenberger2008-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> However, influenza C is less common than the other types and usually only causes mild disease in children.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katagiri_65-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Katagiri-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Influenzavirus_D">Influenzavirus D</span></h4>
<p>This genus has only one species, influenza D virus, which infects pigs and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a>. The virus has the potential to infect humans, although no such cases have been observed.<sup id="cite_ref-InfD2017_9-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-InfD2017-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Structure.2C_properties.2C_and_subtype_nomenclature"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure,_properties,_and_subtype_nomenclature">Structure, properties, and subtype nomenclature</span></h3>
<p>Influenzaviruses A, B, C, and D are very similar in overall structure.<sup id="cite_ref-InfD2017_9-3" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-InfD2017-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The virus particle (also called the virion) can range from a spherical shape 100–120&#160;<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanometer" class="mw-redirect" title="Nanometer">nm</a> in diameter to a long filamentous morphology with an 80–100&#160;nm diameter and a length of up to 20&#160;µm.<sup id="cite_ref-Sugita_2011_68-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Sugita_2011-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pmid27365089_69-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-pmid27365089-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> However, despite these varied shapes, the viral particles of all influenza viruses are similar in composition.<sup id="cite_ref-Bouvier_70-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Bouvier-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> These are made of a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viral_envelope" title="Viral envelope">viral envelope</a> containing the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glycoprotein" title="Glycoprotein">glycoproteins</a> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemagglutinin_(influenza)" title="Hemagglutinin (influenza)">hemagglutinin</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viral_neuraminidase" title="Viral neuraminidase">neuraminidase</a> wrapped around a central core. The central core contains the viral <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> and other viral proteins that package and protect this RNA. RNA tends to be single stranded but in special cases it is double.<sup id="cite_ref-Lamb_71-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Lamb-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Unusually for a virus, its genome is not a single piece of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nucleic_acid" title="Nucleic acid">nucleic acid</a>; instead, it contains seven or eight pieces of segmented <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Negative-sense" class="mw-redirect" title="Negative-sense">negative-sense</a> RNA, each piece of RNA containing either one or two <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a>, which code for a gene product (protein).<sup id="cite_ref-Bouvier_70-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Bouvier-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> For example, the influenza A genome contains eight pieces of RNA, encoding for 11 <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemagglutinin_(influenza)" title="Hemagglutinin (influenza)">hemagglutinin</a> (HA), <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viral_neuraminidase" title="Viral neuraminidase">neuraminidase</a> (NA), <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_virus_nucleoprotein" title="Influenza virus nucleoprotein">nucleoprotein</a> (NP), <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/M1_protein" title="M1 protein">M1</a> (matrix 1 protein), <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/M2_protein" class="mw-redirect" title="M2 protein">M2</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NS1_Influenza_Protein" class="mw-redirect" title="NS1 Influenza Protein">NS1</a> (non-structural protein 1), NS2 (other name is NEP, nuclear export protein), PA, PB1 (polymerase basic 1), PB1-F2 and PB2.<sup id="cite_ref-Ghedin_72-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Ghedin-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemagglutinin_(influenza)" title="Hemagglutinin (influenza)">Hemagglutinin</a> (HA) and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuraminidase" title="Neuraminidase">neuraminidase</a> (NA) are the two large glycoproteins on the outside of the viral particles that serve as key targets for antibodies and antiviral drugs.<sup id="cite_ref-Hilleman_73-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Hilleman-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> HA is a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lectin" title="Lectin">lectin</a> that mediates binding of the virus to target cells and entry of the viral genome into the target cell, while NA is involved in the release of progeny virus from infected cells by cleaving sugars that bind the mature viral particles.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Influenza A viruses are further classified by their subtypes; for example, H3N2 represents a virus with hemagglutinin (H) of subtype 3 and neuraminidase (N) of subtype 2. There are 18 H subtypes and 11 N subtypes, but only H1, H2, or H3 combined with N1 or N2 are commonly found in humans.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Replication">Replication</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Virus_Replication_large.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Virus_Replication_large.svg/310px-Virus_Replication_large.svg.png" decoding="async" width="310" height="286" class="thumbimage" srcset="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Virus_Replication_large.svg/465px-Virus_Replication_large.svg.png 1.5x, https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Virus_Replication_large.svg/620px-Virus_Replication_large.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="925" data-file-height="852" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Virus_Replication_large.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Host cell invasion and replication by the influenza virus. The steps in this process are discussed in the text.</div></div></div>
<p>Influenza primarily infects lung epithelial cells through a mult-step process.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> First, the virus binds to and enters its target cell and then traffics its genome into the cell's nucleus. Inside the nucleus, the viral genome replicates while also producing the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Messenger_RNA" title="Messenger RNA">messenger RNA</a> that exits the nucleus to produce new copies of the viral proteins. These components assemble into new virus particles that bud off the surface of the cell as new infectious virions.<sup id="cite_ref-Bouvier_70-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Bouvier-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To describe this process in more detail, influenza viruses bind through <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemagglutinin" title="Hemagglutinin">hemagglutinin</a> onto <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sialic_acid" title="Sialic acid">sialic acid</a> sugars on the surfaces of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epithelium" title="Epithelium">epithelial cells</a>, typically in the nose, throat, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a> of mammals, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intestine" class="mw-redirect" title="Intestine">intestines</a> of birds (Stage 1 in infection figure).<sup id="cite_ref-Wagner_79-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Wagner-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> After the hemagglutinin is <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proteolysis" title="Proteolysis">cleaved</a> by a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protease" title="Protease">protease</a>, the cell imports the virus by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endocytosis" title="Endocytosis">endocytosis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Steinhauer_80-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Steinhauer-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The intracellular details are still being elucidated. It is known that virions converge to the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microtubule" title="Microtubule">microtubule</a> organizing center, interact with acidic endosomes and finally enter the target endosomes for genome release.<sup id="cite_ref-Liu2011_81-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Liu2011-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Once inside the cell, the acidic conditions in the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endosome" title="Endosome">endosome</a> cause two events to happen: First, part of the hemagglutinin protein fuses the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Viral_envelope" title="Viral envelope">viral envelope</a> with the vacuole's membrane, then the M2 <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ion_channel" title="Ion channel">ion channel</a> allows <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> to move through the viral envelope and acidify the core of the virus, which causes the core to disassemble and release the viral RNA and core proteins.<sup id="cite_ref-Bouvier_70-3" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Bouvier-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The viral RNA (vRNA) molecules, accessory proteins and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA-dependent_RNA_polymerase" title="RNA-dependent RNA polymerase">RNA-dependent RNA polymerase</a> are then released into the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a> (Stage 2).<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The M2 ion channel is blocked by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amantadine" title="Amantadine">amantadine</a> drugs, preventing infection.<sup id="cite_ref-Pinto_83-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Pinto-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These core proteins and vRNA form a complex that is transported into the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">cell nucleus</a>, where the RNA-dependent RNA polymerase begins transcribing complementary positive-sense vRNA (Steps 3a and b).<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The vRNA either is exported into the cytoplasm and translated (step 4) or remains in the nucleus. Newly synthesized viral proteins are either secreted through the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golgi_apparatus" title="Golgi apparatus">Golgi apparatus</a> onto the cell surface (in the case of neuraminidase and hemagglutinin, step 5b) or transported back into the nucleus to bind vRNA and form new viral genome particles (step 5a). Other viral proteins have multiple actions in the host cell, including degrading cellular <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MRNA" class="mw-redirect" title="MRNA">mRNA</a> and using the released <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotides</a> for vRNA synthesis and also inhibiting <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Translation_(biology)" title="Translation (biology)">translation</a> of host-cell mRNAs.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Negative-sense vRNAs that form the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genomes</a> of future viruses, RNA-dependent RNA polymerase, and other viral proteins are assembled into a virion. Hemagglutinin and neuraminidase molecules cluster into a bulge in the cell membrane. The vRNA and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capsid" title="Capsid">viral core</a> proteins leave the nucleus and enter this membrane protrusion (step 6). The mature virus buds off from the cell in a sphere of host <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lipid_bilayer" title="Lipid bilayer">phospholipid membrane</a>, acquiring hemagglutinin and neuraminidase with this membrane coat (step 7).<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> As before, the viruses adhere to the cell through hemagglutinin; the mature viruses detach once their <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuraminidase" title="Neuraminidase">neuraminidase</a> has cleaved sialic acid residues from the host cell.<sup id="cite_ref-Wagner_79-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Wagner-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> After the release of new influenza viruses, the host cell dies.
</p><p>Because of the absence of RNA <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proofreading_(biology)" title="Proofreading (biology)">proofreading</a> enzymes, the RNA-dependent RNA polymerase that copies the viral genome makes an error roughly every 10 thousand nucleotides, which is the approximate length of the influenza vRNA. Hence, many of the newly manufactured influenza viruses contain mutations, and over time this causes the virus to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antigenic_drift" title="Antigenic drift">antigenically drift</a>; for example, the hemagglutinin and neuraminidase of the dominant circulating strains acquire 1–3 amino acid substitutions each year.<sup id="cite_ref-Einav2020_6-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Einav2020-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The separation of the genome into eight separate segments of vRNA allows mixing or <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reassortment" title="Reassortment">reassortment</a> of vRNAs if more than one type of influenza virus infects a single cell. The resulting rapid change in viral genetics produces <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antigenic_shift" title="Antigenic shift">antigenic shifts</a>, which are sudden changes from one antigen to another. These sudden large changes allow the virus to infect new host species and quickly overcome protective immunity.<sup id="cite_ref-Hilleman_73-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Hilleman-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> This is important in the emergence of pandemics, as discussed below in the section on <a href="Influenza#Epidemiology">epidemiology</a>.  Also, when two or more viruses infect a cell, genetic variation may be generated by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homologous_recombination" title="Homologous recombination">homologous recombination</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-De_89-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-De-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>  Homologous recombination can arise during viral genome replication by the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RNA-dependent_RNA_polymerase" title="RNA-dependent RNA polymerase">RNA polymerase</a> switching from one template to another, a process known as copy choice.<sup id="cite_ref-De_89-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-De-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanism">Mechanism</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission">Transmission</span></h3>
<p>When an infected person sneezes or coughs more than half a million virus particles can be spread to those close by.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> In otherwise healthy adults, influenza virus shedding (the time during which a person might be infectious to another person) increases sharply one-half to one day after infection, peaks on day 2 and persists for an average total duration of 5 days—but can persist as long as 9 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Einav2020_6-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Einav2020-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AmericanJournalEpidemiology2008_26-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-AmericanJournalEpidemiology2008-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In those who develop symptoms from experimental infection (only 67% of healthy experimentally infected individuals), symptoms and viral shedding show a similar pattern, but with viral shedding preceding illness by one day.<sup id="cite_ref-AmericanJournalEpidemiology2008_26-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-AmericanJournalEpidemiology2008-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Children are much more infectious than adults and shed virus from just before they develop symptoms until two weeks after infection.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immunodeficiency" title="Immunodeficiency">immunocompromised</a> people, viral shedding can continue for longer than two weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Influenza can be spread in three main ways: by direct transmission (when an infected person sneezes mucus directly into the eyes, nose or mouth of another person); the airborne route (when someone inhales the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Particulate" class="mw-redirect" title="Particulate">aerosols</a> produced by an infected person coughing, sneezing or spitting) and through hand-to-eye, hand-to-nose, or hand-to-mouth transmission, either from contaminated surfaces or from direct personal contact such as a handshake.<sup id="cite_ref-Weber_93-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Weber-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The relative importance of these three modes of transmission is unclear, and they may all contribute to the spread of the virus.<sup id="cite_ref-Brankston2007_10-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Brankston2007-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In the airborne route, the droplets that are small enough for people to inhale are 0.5 to 5<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%CE%9Cm" class="mw-redirect" title="Μm">μm</a> in diameter and inhaling just one droplet might be enough to cause an infection.<sup id="cite_ref-Weber_93-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Weber-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Although a single sneeze releases up to 40,000 droplets,<sup id="cite_ref-Cole_1998_95-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Cole_1998-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> most of these droplets are quite large and will quickly settle out of the air.<sup id="cite_ref-Weber_93-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Weber-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> How long influenza survives in airborne droplets seems to be influenced by the levels of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">humidity</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UV_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="UV radiation">UV radiation</a>, with low humidity and a lack of sunlight in winter aiding its survival;<sup id="cite_ref-Weber_93-3" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Weber-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> ideal conditions can allow it to live for an hour in the atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-Kormouth_96-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Kormouth-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As the influenza virus can persist outside of the body, it can also be transmitted by contaminated surfaces such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Banknotes" class="mw-redirect" title="Banknotes">banknotes</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_97-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Thomas-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> doorknobs, light switches and other household items.<sup id="cite_ref-Merck_30-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Merck-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The length of time the virus will persist on a surface varies, with the virus surviving for one to two days on hard, non-porous surfaces such as plastic or metal, for about fifteen minutes on dry paper tissues, and only five minutes on skin.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore_98-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Moore-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> However, if the virus is present in mucus, this can protect it for longer periods (up to 17<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>days on banknotes).<sup id="cite_ref-Weber_93-4" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Weber-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_97-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Thomas-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Avian influenza viruses can survive indefinitely when frozen.<sup id="cite_ref-cfsph_99-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-cfsph-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> They are inactivated by heating to 56<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>°C (133<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>°F) for a minimum of 60 minutes, as well as by acids (at pH&lt;2).<sup id="cite_ref-cfsph_99-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-cfsph-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:H1N1_versus_H5N1_pathology.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/H1N1_versus_H5N1_pathology.png/310px-H1N1_versus_H5N1_pathology.png" decoding="async" width="310" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/H1N1_versus_H5N1_pathology.png/465px-H1N1_versus_H5N1_pathology.png 1.5x, https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/H1N1_versus_H5N1_pathology.png/620px-H1N1_versus_H5N1_pathology.png 2x" data-file-width="754" data-file-height="552" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:H1N1_versus_H5N1_pathology.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The different sites of infection (shown in red) of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_A_virus_subtype_H1N1" title="Influenza A virus subtype H1N1">seasonal H1N1</a> versus <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_A_virus_subtype_H5N1" title="Influenza A virus subtype H5N1">avian H5N1</a>. This influences their lethality and ability to spread.</div></div></div>
<p>The mechanisms by which influenza infection causes symptoms in humans have been studied intensively. One of the mechanisms is believed to be the inhibition of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adrenocorticotropic_hormone" title="Adrenocorticotropic hormone">adrenocorticotropic hormone</a> (ACTH) resulting in lowered <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cortisol" title="Cortisol">cortisol</a> levels.<sup id="cite_ref-jefferies1998_100-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-jefferies1998-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
Knowing which genes are carried by a particular strain can help predict how well it will infect humans and how severe this infection will be (that is, predict the strain's <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pathophysiology" title="Pathophysiology">pathophysiology</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Taubenberger2008_63-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Taubenberger2008-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Korteweg_101-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Korteweg-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For instance, part of the process that allows influenza viruses to invade cells is the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proteolysis" title="Proteolysis">cleavage</a> of the viral <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemagglutinin" title="Hemagglutinin">hemagglutinin</a> protein by any one of several human <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protease" title="Protease">proteases</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Steinhauer_80-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Steinhauer-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In mild and avirulent viruses, the structure of the hemagglutinin means that it can only be cleaved by proteases found in the throat and lungs, so these viruses cannot infect other tissues. However, in highly virulent strains, such as H5N1, the hemagglutinin can be cleaved by a wide variety of proteases, allowing the virus to spread throughout the body.<sup id="cite_ref-Korteweg_101-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Korteweg-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The viral hemagglutinin protein is responsible for determining both which species a strain can infect and where in the human <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Respiratory_tract" title="Respiratory tract">respiratory tract</a> a strain of influenza will bind.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Strains that are easily transmitted between people have hemagglutinin proteins that bind to receptors in the upper part of the respiratory tract, such as in the nose, throat and mouth. In contrast, the highly lethal H5N1 strain binds to receptors that are mostly found deep in the lungs.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> This difference in the site of infection may be part of the reason why the H5N1 strain causes severe viral pneumonia in the lungs, but is not easily transmitted by people coughing and sneezing.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Common symptoms of the flu such as fever, headaches, and fatigue are the result of the huge amounts of proinflammatory <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chemokine" title="Chemokine">chemokines</a> (such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interferon" title="Interferon">interferon</a> or <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tumor_necrosis_factor-alpha" class="mw-redirect" title="Tumor necrosis factor-alpha">tumor necrosis factor</a>) produced from influenza-infected cells.<sup id="cite_ref-Eccles_35-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Eccles-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In contrast to the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhinovirus" title="Rhinovirus">rhinovirus</a> that causes the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_cold" title="Common cold">common cold</a>, influenza does cause tissue damage, so symptoms are not entirely due to the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammatory response</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> This massive immune response might produce a life-threatening <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cytokine_storm" title="Cytokine storm">cytokine storm</a>. This effect has been proposed to be the cause of the unusual lethality of both the H5N1 avian influenza,<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> and the 1918 pandemic strain.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> However, another possibility is that these large amounts of cytokines are just a result of the massive levels of viral replication produced by these strains, and the immune response does not itself contribute to the disease.<sup id="cite_ref-Beigel_111-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Beigel-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Influenza appears to trigger <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Programmed_cell_death" title="Programmed cell death">programmed cell death</a> (apoptosis).<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaccination">Vaccination</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_vaccine" title="Influenza vaccine">Influenza vaccine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Defense.gov_News_Photo_041028-N-9864S-021.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Defense.gov_News_Photo_041028-N-9864S-021.jpg/310px-Defense.gov_News_Photo_041028-N-9864S-021.jpg" decoding="async" width="310" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Defense.gov_News_Photo_041028-N-9864S-021.jpg/465px-Defense.gov_News_Photo_041028-N-9864S-021.jpg 1.5x, https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Defense.gov_News_Photo_041028-N-9864S-021.jpg/620px-Defense.gov_News_Photo_041028-N-9864S-021.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="1960" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Defense.gov_News_Photo_041028-N-9864S-021.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Giving an influenza vaccination</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_vaccine" title="Influenza vaccine">influenza vaccine</a> is recommended by the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) for high-risk groups, such as pregnant women, children aged less than five years, the elderly, health care workers, and people who have chronic illnesses such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heart_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a>, or are immunocompromised among others.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO_Vaccine_use_113-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO_Vaccine_use-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pmid23210147_114-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-pmid23210147-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The United States <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> (CDC) recommends the influenza vaccine for those aged six months or older who do not have contraindications.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CDC2019-20_12-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-CDC2019-20-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In healthy adults it is modestly effective in decreasing the amount of influenza-like symptoms in a population.<sup id="cite_ref-HealthyAdults.Vaccine.CochraneReview.2018_116-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-HealthyAdults.Vaccine.CochraneReview.2018-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> In healthy children over the age of two years, the vaccine reduces the chances of getting influenza by around two-thirds, while it has not been well studied in children under two years.<sup id="cite_ref-CochraneReview.Vaccine.Children.2018_117-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-CochraneReview.Vaccine.Children.2018-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> In those with <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">chronic obstructive pulmonary disease</a> vaccination reduces exacerbations;<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> it is not clear if it reduces asthma exacerbations.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Evidence supports a lower rate of influenza-like illness in many groups who are immunocompromised such as those with: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, and post organ transplant.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> In those at high risk immunization may reduce the risk of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heart_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Udell-2013_121-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Udell-2013-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Whether immunizing health care workers affects patient outcomes is controversial with some reviews finding insufficient evidence<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> and others finding tentative evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to the high <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutation_rate" title="Mutation rate">mutation rate</a> of the virus, a particular influenza vaccine usually confers protection for no more than a few years. Each year, the World Health Organization predicts which strains of the virus are most likely to be circulating in the next year (see <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Historical_annual_reformulations_of_the_influenza_vaccine" title="Historical annual reformulations of the influenza vaccine">Historical annual reformulations of the influenza vaccine</a>), allowing <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pharmaceutical_company" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmaceutical company">pharmaceutical companies</a> to develop vaccines that will provide the best immunity against these strains.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOrecommendation_126-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHOrecommendation-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The vaccine is reformulated each season for a few specific flu strains but does not include all the strains active in the world during that season. It takes about six months for the manufacturers to formulate and produce the millions of doses required to deal with the seasonal epidemics; occasionally, a new or overlooked strain becomes prominent during that time.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> It is also possible to get infected just before vaccination and get sick with the strain that the vaccine is supposed to prevent, as the vaccine takes about two weeks to become effective.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
Vaccines can cause the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a> to react as if the body were actually being infected, and general infection symptoms (many cold and flu symptoms are just general infection symptoms) can appear, though these symptoms are usually not as severe or long-lasting as influenza. The most dangerous <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adverse_effect" title="Adverse effect">adverse effect</a> is a severe <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allergy" title="Allergy">allergic reaction</a> to either the virus material itself or residues from the hen eggs used to grow the influenza; however, these reactions are extremely rare.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2018 Cochrane review of children in good general health found that the live immunization seemed to lower the risk of getting influenza for the season from 18% to 4%. The inactivated vaccine seemed to lower the risk of getting flu for the season from 30% to 11%. Not enough data was available to draw definite conclusions about serious complications such as pneumonia or hospitalization.<sup id="cite_ref-CochraneReview.Vaccine.Children.2018_117-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-CochraneReview.Vaccine.Children.2018-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For healthy adults, a 2018 Cochrane review showed that vaccines reduced the incidence of lab-confirmed influenza from 2.3% to 0.9%, which constitutes a reduction of risk of approximately 60%. However, for influenza-like illness which is defined as the same symptoms of cough, fever, headache, runny nose, and bodily aches and pains, vaccine reduced the risk from 21.5% to 18.1%. This constitutes a much more modest reduction of risk of approximately 16%. The difference is most probably explained by the fact that over 200 viruses cause the same or similar symptoms as the flu virus.<sup id="cite_ref-HealthyAdults.Vaccine.CochraneReview.2018_116-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-HealthyAdults.Vaccine.CochraneReview.2018-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Another review looked at the effect of short and long term exercise before the vaccine, however, no benefits or harms were recorded.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The cost-effectiveness of seasonal influenza vaccination has been widely evaluated for different groups and in different settings.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> It has generally been found to be a cost-effective intervention, especially in children<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> and the elderly,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> however the results of economic evaluations of influenza vaccination have often been found to be dependent on key assumptions.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infection_control">Infection control</span></h3>
