<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_prevention" title="Influenza prevention">Influenza prevention</a></div>
<p>These are the main ways that influenza spreads
</p>
<ul><li>by direct transmission (when an infected person sneezes mucus directly into the eyes, nose or mouth of another person);</li>
<li>the airborne route (when someone inhales the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Particulate" class="mw-redirect" title="Particulate">aerosols</a> produced by an infected person coughing, sneezing or spitting);</li>
<li>through hand-to-eye, hand-to-nose, or hand-to-mouth transmission, either from contaminated surfaces or from direct personal contact such as a hand-shake.</li></ul>
<p>When vaccines and antiviral medications are limited, non-pharmaceutical interventions are essential to reduce transmission and spread. The lack of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Controlled_studies" class="mw-redirect" title="Controlled studies">controlled studies</a> and rigorous evidence of the effectiveness of some measures has hampered planning decisions and recommendations. Nevertheless, strategies endorsed by experts for all phases of flu outbreaks include hand and respiratory hygiene, self-isolation by symptomatic individuals and the use of face masks by them and their caregivers, surface disinfection, rapid testing and diagnosis, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Contact_tracing" title="Contact tracing">contact tracing</a>. In some cases, other forms of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_distancing" title="Social distancing">social distancing</a> including school closures and travel restrictions are recommended.<sup id="cite_ref-Aledort_136-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Aledort-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Reasonably effective ways to reduce the transmission of influenza include good personal health and hygiene habits such as: not touching the eyes, nose or mouth;<sup id="cite_ref-CDCQA_137-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-CDCQA-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> frequent <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hand_washing" title="Hand washing">hand washing</a> (with soap and water, or with alcohol-based hand rubs);<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> covering coughs and sneezes with a tissue or sleeve; avoiding close contact with sick people; and staying home when sick. Avoiding spitting is also recommended.<sup id="cite_ref-Aledort_136-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Aledort-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Although <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surgical_mask" title="Surgical mask">face masks</a> might help prevent transmission when caring for the sick,<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> there is mixed evidence on beneficial effects in the community.<sup id="cite_ref-Aledort_136-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Aledort-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> Smoking raises the risk of contracting influenza, as well as producing more severe disease symptoms.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid16231688_142-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-pmid16231688-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since influenza spreads through both <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Particulate" class="mw-redirect" title="Particulate">aerosols</a> and contact with contaminated surfaces, surface sanitizing may help prevent some infections.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">Alcohol</a> is an effective sanitizer against influenza viruses, while <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quaternary_ammonium_cation" title="Quaternary ammonium cation">quaternary ammonium compounds</a> can be used with alcohol so that the sanitizing effect lasts for longer.<sup id="cite_ref-McDonnell_145-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-McDonnell-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> In hospitals, quaternary ammonium compounds and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bleach" title="Bleach">bleach</a> are used to sanitize rooms or equipment that have been occupied by people with influenza symptoms.<sup id="cite_ref-McDonnell_145-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-McDonnell-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> At home, this can be done effectively with a diluted chlorine bleach.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Social distancing strategies used during past pandemics, such as quarantines, travel restrictions, and the closing of schools, churches and theaters, have been employed to slow the spread of influenza viruses. Researchers have estimated that such interventions during the 1918 Spanish flu pandemic in the US reduced the peak death rate by up to 50%, and the overall mortality by about 10–30%, in areas where multiple interventions were implemented. The more moderate effect on total deaths was attributed to the measures being employed too late, or lifted too early, most after six weeks or less.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For typical flu outbreaks, routine cancellation of large gatherings or mandatory travel restrictions have received little agreement, particularly as they may be disruptive and unpopular. School closures have been found by most empirical studies to reduce community spread, but some findings have been contradictory. Recommendations for these community restrictions are usually on a case-by-case basis.<sup id="cite_ref-Aledort_136-3" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Aledort-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Flu.png.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Flu.png.jpg/310px-Flu.png.jpg" decoding="async" width="310" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Flu.png.jpg/465px-Flu.png.jpg 1.5x, https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Flu.png.jpg/620px-Flu.png.jpg 2x" data-file-width="632" data-file-height="483" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Flu.png.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>29 yr old with H1N1</div></div></div>
<p>There are a number of rapid tests for the flu. One is called a Rapid Molecular Assay, when an upper respiratory tract specimen (mucus) is taken using a nasal swab or a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nasopharyngeal_swab" title="Nasopharyngeal swab">nasopharyngeal swab</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> It should be done within 3–4 days of symptom onset, as upper respiratory viral shedding takes a downward spiral after that.<sup id="cite_ref-cdc.gov_45-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-cdc.gov-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_treatment" title="Influenza treatment">Influenza treatment</a></div>
<p>People with the flu are advised to get plenty of rest, drink plenty of liquids, avoid using <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alcoholic_beverage" class="mw-redirect" title="Alcoholic beverage">alcohol</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">tobacco</a> and, if necessary, take medications such as acetaminophen (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paracetamol" title="Paracetamol">paracetamol</a>) to relieve the fever and muscle aches associated with the flu.<sup id="cite_ref-CDC2010Flu_150-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-CDC2010Flu-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MLP2010_151-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-MLP2010-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, there is not enough evidence to support corticosteroids as additional therapy for influenza.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> It is advised to avoid close contact with others to prevent spread of infection.<sup id="cite_ref-CDC2010Flu_150-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-CDC2010Flu-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MLP2010_151-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-MLP2010-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Children and teenagers with flu symptoms (particularly fever) should avoid taking <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aspirin" title="Aspirin">aspirin</a> during an influenza infection (especially <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenzavirus_B" class="mw-redirect" title="Influenzavirus B">influenza type B</a>), because doing so can lead to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reye%27s_syndrome" class="mw-redirect" title="Reye&#39;s syndrome">Reye's syndrome</a>, a rare but potentially fatal disease of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Since influenza is caused by a virus, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> have no effect on the infection, but may be prescribed for <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secondary_infection" class="mw-redirect" title="Secondary infection">secondary infections</a> such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacterial_pneumonia" title="Bacterial pneumonia">bacterial pneumonia</a>. Antiviral medication may be effective, if given early (within 48 hours to first symptoms), but some strains of influenza can show resistance to the standard antiviral medications and there is concern about the quality of the research.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antivirals">Antivirals</span></h3>
<p>The two classes of antiviral medications used against influenza are <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuraminidase_inhibitor" title="Neuraminidase inhibitor">neuraminidase inhibitors</a> (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oseltamivir" title="Oseltamivir">oseltamivir</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zanamivir" title="Zanamivir">zanamivir</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laninamivir" title="Laninamivir">laninamivir</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peramivir" title="Peramivir">peramivir</a>) and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/M2_protein" class="mw-redirect" title="M2 protein">M2 protein</a> inhibitors (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adamantane" title="Adamantane">adamantane</a> derivatives).<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> In Russia, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Umifenovir" title="Umifenovir">umifenovir</a> is sold for treatment of influenza<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> and in the first quarter of 2020 had a 16 percent share in the antiviral market.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Neuraminidase_inhibitors">Neuraminidase inhibitors</span></h4>
<p>Overall the benefits of neuraminidase inhibitors in those who are otherwise healthy do not appear to be greater than the risks.<sup id="cite_ref-Mich2013_13-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Mich2013-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> There does not appear to be any benefit in those with other health problems.<sup id="cite_ref-Mich2013_13-3" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Mich2013-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In those believed to have the flu, they decreased the length of time symptoms were present by slightly less than a day but did not appear to affect the risk of complications such as needing hospitalization or <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ebe2013_14-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Ebe2013-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Increasingly prevalent resistance to neuraminidase inhibitors has led researchers to seek alternative antiviral medications with different mechanisms of action.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="M2_inhibitors">M2 inhibitors</span></h4>
<p>The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiviral_drug" title="Antiviral drug">antiviral medications</a> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amantadine" title="Amantadine">amantadine</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rimantadine" title="Rimantadine">rimantadine</a> inhibit a viral <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ion_channel" title="Ion channel">ion channel</a> (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/M2_protein" class="mw-redirect" title="M2 protein">M2 protein</a>), thus inhibiting replication of the influenza A virus.<sup id="cite_ref-Pinto_83-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Pinto-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> These medications are sometimes effective against influenza A if given early in the infection but are ineffective against influenza B viruses, which lack the M2 drug target.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> Measured resistance to amantadine and rimantadine in American isolates of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H3N2" class="mw-redirect" title="H3N2">H3N2</a> has increased to 91% in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> This high level of resistance may be due to the easy availability of amantadines as part of over-the-counter cold remedies in countries such as China and Russia,<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> and their use to prevent outbreaks of influenza in farmed poultry.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> The CDC recommended against using M2 inhibitors during the 2005–06 influenza season due to high levels of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drug_resistance" title="Drug resistance">drug resistance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span></h2>
<p>Influenza's effects are much more severe and last longer than those of the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_cold" title="Common cold">common cold</a>. Most people will recover completely in about one to two weeks, but others will develop life-threatening complications (such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>). Thus, influenza can be deadly, especially for the weak, young and old, those with compromised immune systems, or the chronically ill.<sup id="cite_ref-Hilleman_73-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Hilleman-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> People with a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immunosuppression" title="Immunosuppression">weak immune system</a>, such as people with advanced <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> infection or transplant recipients (whose immune systems are medically suppressed to prevent transplant organ rejection), suffer from particularly severe disease.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Pregnant women and young children are also at a high risk for complications.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The flu can worsen chronic health problems. People with emphysema, chronic bronchitis or asthma may experience <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dyspnea" class="mw-redirect" title="Dyspnea">shortness of breath</a> while they have the flu, and influenza may cause worsening of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coronary_heart_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronary heart disease">coronary heart disease</a> or <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Congestive_heart_failure" class="mw-redirect" title="Congestive heart failure">congestive heart failure</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">Smoking</a> is another <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Risk_factor" title="Risk factor">risk factor</a> associated with more serious disease and increased mortality from influenza.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid16231688_142-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-pmid16231688-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Even healthy people can be affected, and serious problems from influenza can happen at any age. People over 65 years old, pregnant women, very young children and people of any age with chronic <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medical_conditions" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical conditions">medical conditions</a> are more likely to get complications from influenza, such as pneumonia, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bronchitis" title="Bronchitis">bronchitis</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paranasal_sinus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paranasal sinus">sinus</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ear_infection" class="mw-redirect" title="Ear infection">ear infections</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neurological_complications">Neurological complications</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Influenza_encephalitis_MRI.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Influenza_encephalitis_MRI.jpg/220px-Influenza_encephalitis_MRI.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Influenza_encephalitis_MRI.jpg/330px-Influenza_encephalitis_MRI.jpg 1.5x, https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Influenza_encephalitis_MRI.jpg/440px-Influenza_encephalitis_MRI.jpg 2x" data-file-width="787" data-file-height="1048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Influenza_encephalitis_MRI.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Influenza encephalitis MRI</div></div></div>
<p>In some cases, an <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autoimmune" class="mw-redirect" title="Autoimmune">autoimmune</a> response to an influenza infection may contribute to the development of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guillain%E2%80%93Barr%C3%A9_syndrome" title="Guillain–Barré syndrome">Guillain–Barré syndrome</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sivadon-Tardy_171-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Sivadon-Tardy-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> However, as many other infections can increase the risk of this disease, influenza may only be an important cause during epidemics.<sup id="cite_ref-Sivadon-Tardy_171-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Sivadon-Tardy-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> This syndrome has been believed to also be a rare side effect of influenza vaccines. One review gives an incidence of about one case per million vaccinations.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Getting infected by influenza itself increases both the risk of death (up to 1 in 10,000) and increases the risk of developing GBS to a much higher level than the highest level of suspected vaccine involvement (approx. 10 times higher by recent estimates).<sup id="cite_ref-Sivadon-Tardy_171-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Sivadon-Tardy-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC), "Children of any age with neurologic conditions are more likely than other children to become very sick if they get the flu. Flu complications may vary and for some children, can include pneumonia and even death."<sup id="cite_ref-CDC_neurologic-pediatric_175-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-CDC_neurologic-pediatric-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Neurological conditions can include:
</p>
<ul><li>Disorders of the brain and spinal cord</li>
<li>Cerebral palsy</li>
<li>Epilepsy (seizure disorders)</li>
<li>Stroke</li>
<li>Intellectual disability</li>
<li>Moderate to severe developmental delay</li>
<li>Muscular dystrophy</li>
<li>Spinal cord injury</li></ul>
<p>These conditions can impair coughing, swallowing, clearing the airways, and in the worst cases, breathing. Therefore, they worsen the flu symptoms.<sup id="cite_ref-CDC_neurologic-pediatric_175-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-CDC_neurologic-pediatric-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seasonal_variations">Seasonal variations</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flu_season" title="Flu season">Flu season</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_influenza_statistics_by_flu_season" title="United States influenza statistics by flu season">United States influenza statistics by flu season</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Influenza_Seasonal_Risk_Areas.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Influenza_Seasonal_Risk_Areas.svg/310px-Influenza_Seasonal_Risk_Areas.svg.png" decoding="async" width="310" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Influenza_Seasonal_Risk_Areas.svg/465px-Influenza_Seasonal_Risk_Areas.svg.png 1.5x, https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Influenza_Seasonal_Risk_Areas.svg/620px-Influenza_Seasonal_Risk_Areas.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="863" data-file-height="443" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Influenza_Seasonal_Risk_Areas.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seasonal risk areas for influenza: November–April (blue), April–November (red), and year-round (yellow).</div></div></div>
<p>Influenza reaches peak prevalence in winter, and because the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemispheres</a> have winter at different times of the year, there are actually two different flu seasons each year. This is why the World Health Organization (assisted by the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Influenza_Centers" title="National Influenza Centers">National Influenza Centers</a>) makes recommendations for two different vaccine formulations every year; one for the Northern, and one for the Southern Hemisphere.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOrecommendation_126-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHOrecommendation-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A long-standing puzzle has been why outbreaks of the flu occur seasonally rather than uniformly throughout the year. One possible explanation is that, because people are indoors more often during the winter, they are in close contact more often, and this promotes transmission from person to person. Increased travel due to the Northern Hemisphere winter holiday season may also play a role.<sup id="cite_ref-NPR2003-12-17_176-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-NPR2003-12-17-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Another factor is that cold temperatures lead to drier air, which may dehydrate mucus particles. Dry particles are lighter and can thus remain airborne for a longer period. The virus also survives longer on surfaces at colder temperatures and aerosol transmission of the virus is highest in cold environments (less than 5<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>°C) with low relative humidity.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> The lower air humidity in winter seems to be the main cause of seasonal influenza transmission in temperate regions.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, seasonal changes in infection rates also occur in tropical regions, and in some countries these peaks of infection are seen mainly during the rainy season.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Seasonal changes in contact rates from school terms, which are a major factor in other <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_childhood_diseases" class="mw-redirect" title="List of childhood diseases">childhood diseases</a> such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pertussis" class="mw-redirect" title="Pertussis">pertussis</a>, may also play a role in the flu. A combination of these small seasonal effects may be amplified by dynamical resonance with the endogenous disease cycles.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H5N1" class="mw-redirect" title="H5N1">H5N1</a> exhibits seasonality in both humans and birds.<sup id="cite_ref-WHOH5N1data_182-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHOH5N1data-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An alternative hypothesis to explain seasonality in influenza infections is an effect of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vitamin_D_and_influenza" class="mw-redirect" title="Vitamin D and influenza">vitamin D</a> levels on immunity to the virus.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> This idea was first proposed by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_Edgar_Hope-Simpson" title="Robert Edgar Hope-Simpson">Robert Edgar Hope-Simpson</a> in 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-Hope-Simpson,_Journal_of_Hygiene_1981_185-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Hope-Simpson,_Journal_of_Hygiene_1981-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> He proposed that the cause of influenza epidemics during winter may be connected to seasonal fluctuations of vitamin D, which is produced in the skin under the influence of solar (or artificial) <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">UV radiation</a>. This could explain why influenza occurs mostly in winter and during the tropical rainy season, when people stay indoors, away from the sun, and their vitamin D levels fall.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)"><span title="Material near this tag needs references to reliable medical sources. (January 2021)">medical citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mortality">Mortality</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:InfluenzaCaseMortality.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/InfluenzaCaseMortality.svg/350px-InfluenzaCaseMortality.svg.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="289" class="thumbimage" srcset="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/InfluenzaCaseMortality.svg/525px-InfluenzaCaseMortality.svg.png 1.5x, https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/InfluenzaCaseMortality.svg/700px-InfluenzaCaseMortality.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="248" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:InfluenzaCaseMortality.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Influenza mortality in symptomatic cases in the US for the 2018/2019 season. The Y axis goes to 1%.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Every year about 290,000 to 650,000 people die due to influenza globally, with an average of 389,000.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In the developed world most of those who die are over the age of 65.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-17" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> In the developing world the effects are less clear; however, it appears that children are affected to a greater degree.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-18" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the number of cases of influenza can vary widely between years, approximately 36,000 deaths and more than 200,000 hospitalizations are directly associated with influenza a year in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> One method of calculating influenza mortality produced an estimate of 41,400 average deaths per year in the United States between 1979 and 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Different methods in 2010 by the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a> (CDC) reported a range from a low of about 3,300 deaths to a high of 49,000 per year.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outbreaks">Outbreaks</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flu_pandemic" class="mw-redirect" title="Flu pandemic">Flu pandemic</a></div>
<p>As influenza is caused by a variety of species and strains of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>, in any given year some strains can die out while others create <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epidemic" title="Epidemic">epidemics</a>, while yet another strain can cause a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemic</a>. Typically, in a year's normal two <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flu_season" title="Flu season">flu seasons</a> (one per hemisphere), there are between three and five million cases of severe illness,<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-19" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WHO2017_4-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2017-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Loz2012_192-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Loz2012-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> which by some definitions is a yearly influenza epidemic.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2018_1-20" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-WHO2018-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Roughly three times per century, a pandemic occurs, which infects a large proportion of the world's population and can kill tens of millions of people (see <a href="Influenza#Pandemics" title="Influenza">pandemics section</a>). In 2006, a study estimated that if a strain with similar <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virulence" title="Virulence">virulence</a> to the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1918_flu_pandemic" class="mw-redirect" title="1918 flu pandemic">1918 influenza</a> had emerged that year, it could have killed between 50 and 80<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million people.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Influenza_geneticshift.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Influenza_geneticshift.svg/220px-Influenza_geneticshift.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Influenza_geneticshift.svg/330px-Influenza_geneticshift.svg.png 1.5x, https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Influenza_geneticshift.svg/440px-Influenza_geneticshift.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="426" data-file-height="520" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Influenza_geneticshift.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Antigenic shift, or reassortment, can result in novel and highly pathogenic strains of human influenza</div></div></div>
<p>New influenza viruses are constantly <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolving</a> by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutation</a> or by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reassortment" title="Reassortment">reassortment</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hay_51-3" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-hay-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Mutations can cause small changes in the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemagglutinin" title="Hemagglutinin">hemagglutinin</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuraminidase" title="Neuraminidase">neuraminidase</a> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigens</a> on the surface of the virus. This is called <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antigenic_drift" title="Antigenic drift">antigenic drift</a>, which slowly creates an increasing variety of strains until one evolves that can infect people who are immune to the pre-existing strains. This new variant then replaces the older strains as it rapidly sweeps through the human population, often causing an epidemic.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> However, since the strains produced by drift will still be reasonably similar to the older strains, some people will still be immune to them. In contrast, when influenza viruses reassort, they acquire completely new antigens—for example by reassortment between avian strains and human strains; this is called <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antigenic_shift" title="Antigenic shift">antigenic shift</a>. If a human influenza virus is produced that has entirely new antigens, everybody will be susceptible, and the novel influenza will spread uncontrollably, causing a pandemic.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> In contrast to this model of pandemics based on antigenic drift and shift, an alternative approach has been proposed where the periodic pandemics are produced by interactions of a fixed set of viral strains with a human population with a constantly changing set of immunities to different viral strains.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ferguson_influenza_generation_time_distribution.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Ferguson_influenza_generation_time_distribution.png/220px-Ferguson_influenza_generation_time_distribution.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Ferguson_influenza_generation_time_distribution.png/330px-Ferguson_influenza_generation_time_distribution.png 1.5x, https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Ferguson_influenza_generation_time_distribution.png/440px-Ferguson_influenza_generation_time_distribution.png 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="450" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ferguson_influenza_generation_time_distribution.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The generation time for influenza (the time from one infection to the next) is very short (only 2 days). This explains why influenza epidemics start and finish in a short time scale of only a few months.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson2005_197-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Ferguson2005-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>From a public health point of view, flu epidemics spread rapidly and are very difficult to control. Most influenza virus strains are not very infectious and each infected individual will only go on to infect one or two other individuals (the basic reproduction number for influenza is generally around 1.4). However, the generation time for influenza is extremely short: the time from a person becoming infected to when he infects the next person is only two days. The short generation time means that influenza epidemics generally peak at around 2 months and burn out after 3 months: the decision to intervene in an influenza epidemic, therefore, has to be taken early, and the decision is therefore often made on the back of incomplete data. Another problem is that individuals become infectious before they become symptomatic, which means that putting people in quarantine after they become ill is not an effective public health intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson2005_197-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Ferguson2005-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> For the average person, viral shedding tends to peak on day two, whereas symptoms peak on day three.<sup id="cite_ref-AmericanJournalEpidemiology2008_26-3" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-AmericanJournalEpidemiology2008-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h3>
<p>The word <i>Influenza</i> comes from the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian language</a> meaning "influence" and refers to the cause of the disease; initially, this ascribed illness to unfavorable <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrological</a> influences. It was introduced into English in the mid-eighteenth century during a pan-European epidemic.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>
Archaic terms for influenza include <i>epidemic catarrh</i>, <i>la grippe</i> (from the French, first used by Molyneaux in 1694; also used in German),<sup id="cite_ref-Potter_199-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Potter-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> <i>sweating sickness</i>, and <i>Spanish fever</i> (particularly for the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1918_flu_pandemic" class="mw-redirect" title="1918 flu pandemic">1918 flu pandemic</a> strain).<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pandemics">Pandemics</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_pandemic" title="Influenza pandemic">Influenza pandemic</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spanish_flu" title="Spanish flu">Spanish flu</a>, and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1968_flu_pandemic" class="mw-redirect" title="1968 flu pandemic">Hong Kong flu</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:W_curve.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/W_curve.png/220px-W_curve.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/W_curve.png/330px-W_curve.png 1.5x, https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/W_curve.png/440px-W_curve.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="424" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:W_curve.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The difference between the influenza mortality age distributions of the 1918 epidemic and normal epidemics. Deaths per 100,000 persons in each age group, United States, for the interpandemic years 1911–1917 (dashed line) and the pandemic year 1918 (solid line).<sup id="cite_ref-Taubenberger_201-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Taubenberger-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg/220px-Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg/330px-Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg 1.5x, https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg/440px-Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Airport_Thermographic_Camera.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thermal imaging camera and screen, photographed in an airport terminal in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> during the 2009 flu pandemic. Thermal imaging can detect elevated body temperature, one of the signs of swine flu.</div></div></div>
<p>An overall lack of data up until 1500 precludes meaningful search for the influenza outbreaks in the more distant past.<sup id="cite_ref-flu_202-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-flu-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Possibly the first influenza pandemic occurred around 6000 BC in China.<sup id="cite_ref-flu_202-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-flu-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> The symptoms of human influenza were clearly described by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a> roughly 2,400 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> Although the virus seems to have caused epidemics throughout human history, historical data on influenza are difficult to interpret, because the symptoms can be similar to those of other respiratory diseases.<sup id="cite_ref-Potter_199-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Potter-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> The disease may have spread from Europe to the Americas as early as the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="European colonization of the Americas">European colonization of the Americas</a>, since almost the entire indigenous population of the Antilles was killed by an epidemic resembling influenza that broke out in 1493, after the arrival of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first convincing record of an influenza pandemic was a minor pandemic <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1510_influenza_pandemic" title="1510 influenza pandemic">chronicled in 1510</a>, which began in East Asia before spreading to North Africa and then Europe. During this pandemic, influenza killed about 1% of its victims.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> The first pandemic of influenza to be reliably recorded as spreading worldwide was the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1557_influenza_pandemic" title="1557 influenza pandemic">1557 influenza pandemic</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-:17_210-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-:17-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:00_211-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-:00-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:3_212-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-:3-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:4_213-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-:4-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> in which a reoccurring wave likely killed <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Queen Mary I of England</a> and the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reginald_Pole" title="Reginald Pole">Archbishop of Canterbury</a> within 12 hours of each other.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title="&#160;Dead link since January 2021">dead link</span></a></i>&#93;</span></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (January 2021)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-:13_214-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-:13-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> One of the most well-chronicled pandemics of influenza in the 16th Century occurred in 1580, beginning in East Asia and spreading to Europe through Africa, Russia, and the Spanish and Ottoman Empires. In <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_Rome#Renaissance_Rome" title="History of Rome">Rome</a>, over 8,000 people were killed. Several Spanish cities saw large scale deaths, among the fatalities the Queen of Spain, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anna_of_Austria,_Queen_of_Spain" title="Anna of Austria, Queen of Spain">Anna of Austria</a>. Pandemics continued sporadically throughout the 17th and 18th centuries, with the pandemic of 1830–1833 being particularly widespread; it infected approximately a quarter of the people exposed.<sup id="cite_ref-Potter_199-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Potter-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most lethal outbreak was the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1918_flu_pandemic" class="mw-redirect" title="1918 flu pandemic">1918 flu pandemic</a> (Spanish flu) (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenzavirus_A" class="mw-redirect" title="Influenzavirus A">type A influenza</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H1N1" class="mw-redirect" title="H1N1">H1N1</a> subtype), which lasted into 1920. It is not known exactly how many it killed, but estimates range from 17 million to 100<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million people.<sup id="cite_ref-Spreeuwenberg1_18-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Spreeuwenberg1-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Taubenberger_201-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Taubenberger-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Knobler_216-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Knobler-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Patterson1_217-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Patterson1-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> This pandemic has been described as "the greatest medical holocaust in history" and may have killed as many people as the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Potter_199-3" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Potter-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> This huge death toll was caused by an extremely high infection rate of up to 50% and the extreme severity of the symptoms, suspected to be caused by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cytokine_storm" title="Cytokine storm">cytokine storms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Patterson1_217-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Patterson1-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> Symptoms in 1918 were so unusual that initially influenza was misdiagnosed as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dengue" class="mw-redirect" title="Dengue">dengue</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a>, or typhoid. One observer wrote, "One of the most striking of the complications was hemorrhage from <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mucous_membrane" title="Mucous membrane">mucous membranes</a>, especially from the nose, stomach, and intestine. Bleeding from the ears and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petechia" title="Petechia">petechial</a> hemorrhages in the skin also occurred."<sup id="cite_ref-Knobler_216-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Knobler-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> The majority of deaths were from <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacterial_pneumonia" title="Bacterial pneumonia">bacterial pneumonia</a>, a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secondary_infection" class="mw-redirect" title="Secondary infection">secondary infection</a> caused by influenza, but the virus also killed people directly, causing massive <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bleeding" title="Bleeding">hemorrhages</a> and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edema" title="Edema">edema</a> in the lung.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_218-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-autogenerated1-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1918 flu pandemic was truly global, spreading even to the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> and remote <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacific Islands">Pacific islands</a>. The unusually severe disease killed between two and twenty percent of those infected, as opposed to the more usual flu epidemic <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">mortality rate</a> of 0.1%.<sup id="cite_ref-Taubenberger_201-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Taubenberger-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Knobler_216-2" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Knobler-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> Another unusual feature of this pandemic was that it mostly killed young adults, with 99% of pandemic influenza deaths occurring in people under 65, and more than half in young adults 20 to 40 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> This is unusual since influenza is normally most deadly to the very young (under age 2) and the very old (over age 70). The total mortality of the 1918–1919 pandemic is not known, but it is estimated that 2.5% to 5% of the world's population was killed. As many as 25<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million may have been killed in the first 25 weeks; in contrast, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AIDS" class="mw-redirect" title="AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a> has killed 25<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million in its first 25 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Knobler_216-3" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Knobler-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Later flu pandemics were not so devastating. They included the 1957 <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asian_Flu" class="mw-redirect" title="Asian Flu">Asian flu</a> (type A, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H2N2" class="mw-redirect" title="H2N2">H2N2</a> strain), the 1968 <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hong_Kong_Flu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hong Kong Flu">Hong Kong flu</a> (type A, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H3N2" class="mw-redirect" title="H3N2">H3N2</a> strain) and the 1977 <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1977_Russian_flu" title="1977 Russian flu">Russian flu</a> (type A, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H1n1" class="mw-redirect" title="H1n1">H1N1</a> strain), but even these smaller outbreaks killed millions of people. In later pandemics <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> were available to control secondary infections and this may have helped reduce mortality compared to the Spanish flu of 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-Taubenberger_201-3" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Taubenberger-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><br />
</p>

<tr>
<td colspan="9" style="text-align:left;"><b>Notes</b>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-240"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="Influenza#cite_ref-240">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Not pandemic, but included for comparison purposes.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><br />
It was incorrectly assumed that the cause of influenza was bacterial in origin from 1892 (with <i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haemophilus_influenzae" title="Haemophilus influenzae">Haemophilus influenzae</a></i> being discovered by and suggested as the origin of influenza by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Friedrich_Johannes_Pfeiffer" title="Richard Friedrich Johannes Pfeiffer">R. F. J. Pfeiffer</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Tbnbrgrtl_246-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Tbnbrgrtl-246">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> The first influenza virus to be isolated was from poultry, when in 1901, the agent causing a disease called "fowl plague" was passed through <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chamberland_filter" title="Chamberland filter">Chamberland filters</a>, which have pores that are too small for <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> to pass through.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-247">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> However, the conceptual differences between viruses and bacteria as different entities was not fully understood for some time, complicating preventive measures taken during the 1918 influenza pandemic.<sup id="cite_ref-Tbnbrgrtl_246-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Tbnbrgrtl-246">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Etiology" title="Etiology">etiological</a> cause of influenza, the virus family Orthomyxoviridae, was first discovered in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">pigs</a> by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Shope" title="Richard Shope">Richard Shope</a> in 1931.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-248">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> This discovery was shortly followed by the isolation of the virus from humans by a group headed by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Patrick_Laidlaw" title="Patrick Laidlaw">Patrick Laidlaw</a> at the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medical_Research_Council_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Medical Research Council (UK)">Medical Research Council</a> of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">the United Kingdom</a> in 1933.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-249">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> However, it was not until <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wendell_Stanley" class="mw-redirect" title="Wendell Stanley">Wendell Stanley</a> first crystallized <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tobacco_mosaic_virus" title="Tobacco mosaic virus">tobacco mosaic virus</a> in 1935 that the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">non-cellular</a> nature of viruses was appreciated.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(medicine)" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (medicine)"><span title="Material near this tag needs references to reliable medical sources. (January 2021)">medical citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Influenza_subtypes.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Influenza_subtypes.svg/350px-Influenza_subtypes.svg.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Influenza_subtypes.svg/525px-Influenza_subtypes.svg.png 1.5x, https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Influenza_subtypes.svg/700px-Influenza_subtypes.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="631" data-file-height="250" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Influenza_subtypes.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The main types of influenza viruses in humans. Solid squares show the appearance of a new strain, causing recurring influenza pandemics. Broken lines indicate uncertain strain identifications.<sup id="cite_ref-Palese_250-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Palese-250">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The first significant step towards preventing influenza was the development in 1944 of a killed-virus vaccine for influenza by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Francis,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Francis, Jr.">Thomas Francis, Jr.</a> This built on work by Australian <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frank_Macfarlane_Burnet" class="mw-redirect" title="Frank Macfarlane Burnet">Frank Macfarlane Burnet</a>, who showed that the virus lost virulence when it was cultured in fertilized hen's eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-Nobel_251-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Nobel-251">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> Application of this observation by Francis allowed his group of researchers at the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a> to develop the first influenza vaccine, with support from the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">U.S. Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-252">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> The Army was deeply involved in this research due to its experience of influenza in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, when thousands of troops were killed by the virus in a matter of months.<sup id="cite_ref-Knobler_216-4" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Knobler-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> In comparison to vaccines, the development of anti-influenza drugs has been slower, with <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amantadine" title="Amantadine">amantadine</a> being licensed in 1966 and, almost thirty years later, the next class of drugs (the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuraminidase_inhibitor" title="Neuraminidase inhibitor">neuraminidase inhibitors</a>) being developed.<sup id="cite_ref-Lynch_253-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Lynch-253">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_impact_of_H5N1" class="mw-redirect" title="Social impact of H5N1">Social impact of H5N1</a></div>
<p>Influenza produces <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Variable_cost" title="Variable cost">direct costs</a> due to lost <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Productivity" title="Productivity">productivity</a> and associated medical treatment, as well as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indirect_costs" title="Indirect costs">indirect costs</a> of preventive measures. In the United States, seasonal influenza is estimated to result in a total average annual economic cost of over $11 billion, with direct medical costs estimated to be over $3<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>billion annually.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-254">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> It has been estimated that a future pandemic could cause hundreds of billions of dollars in direct and indirect costs.<sup id="cite_ref-Bush_255-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Bush-255">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> However, the economic impacts of past pandemics have not been intensively studied, and some authors have suggested that the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spanish_flu" title="Spanish flu">Spanish influenza</a> actually had a positive long-term effect on per-capita income growth, despite a large reduction in the working population and severe short-term <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recession" title="Recession">depressive</a> effects.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-256">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> Other studies have attempted to predict the costs of a pandemic as serious as the 1918 Spanish flu on the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economy_of_the_United_States" title="Economy of the United States">U.S. economy</a>, where 30% of all workers became ill, and 2.5% were killed. A 30% sickness rate and a three-week length of illness would decrease the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> by 5%. Additional costs would come from medical treatment of 18<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million to 45<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million people, and total economic costs would be approximately $700<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>billion.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-257">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Preventive costs are also high. Governments worldwide have spent billions of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">U.S. dollars</a> preparing and planning for a potential H5N1 avian influenza pandemic, with costs associated with purchasing drugs and vaccines as well as developing <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emergency_management" title="Emergency management">disaster drills</a> and strategies for improved <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Border_control" title="Border control">border controls</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rosenthal_258-0" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Rosenthal-258">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> On 1 November 2005, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">United States President</a> <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> unveiled the National Strategy to Safeguard Against the Danger of Pandemic Influenza<sup id="cite_ref-Bush_255-1" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-Bush-255">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> backed by a request to <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> for $7.1<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>billion to begin implementing the plan.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-259">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> Internationally, on 18 January 2006, donor nations pledged US$2<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>billion to combat bird flu at the two-day International Pledging Conference on Avian and Human Influenza held in China.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-260">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-261">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In an assessment of the 2009 H1N1 pandemic on selected countries in the Southern Hemisphere, data suggest that all countries experienced some time-limited and/or geographically isolated socioeconomic effects and a temporary decrease in tourism most likely due to fear of 2009 H1N1 disease. It is still too early to determine whether the H1N1 pandemic has had any long-term economic effects.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-262">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (February 2020)">needs update</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_research" title="Influenza research">Influenza research</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Influenza_virus_research.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Influenza_virus_research.jpg/170px-Influenza_virus_research.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Influenza_virus_research.jpg/255px-Influenza_virus_research.jpg 1.5x, https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Influenza_virus_research.jpg/340px-Influenza_virus_research.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1966" data-file-height="2786" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Influenza_virus_research.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dr. Terrence Tumpey examining a laboratory-grown reconstruction of the 1918 <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spanish_flu" title="Spanish flu">Spanish flu</a> virus in a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biosafety_level" title="Biosafety level">biosafety level</a> 3 environment.</div></div></div>
<p>Research on influenza includes studies on <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_virology" title="Molecular virology">molecular virology</a>, how the virus produces disease (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pathogenesis" title="Pathogenesis">pathogenesis</a>), host <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immune_response" title="Immune response">immune responses</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genomics" title="Genomics">viral genomics</a>, and how the virus spreads (<a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epidemiology" title="Epidemiology">epidemiology</a>). These studies help in developing influenza countermeasures; for example, a better understanding of the body's immune system response helps <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccine</a> development, and a detailed picture of how influenza invades cells aids the development of antiviral drugs. One important <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Research" title="Research">basic research</a> program is the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Influenza_Genome_Sequencing_Project" title="Influenza Genome Sequencing Project">Influenza Genome Sequencing Project</a>, which was initiated in 2004 to create a library of influenza sequences and help clarify which factors make one strain more lethal than another, which genes most affect <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immunogenicity" title="Immunogenicity">immunogenicity</a>, and how the virus <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolves</a> over time.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-263">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The sequencing of the influenza genome and <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recombinant_DNA" title="Recombinant DNA">recombinant DNA</a> technology may accelerate the generation of new vaccine strains by allowing scientists to substitute new antigens into a previously developed vaccine strain.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-264">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> Growing viruses in <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_culture" title="Cell culture">cell culture</a> also promises higher yields, less cost, better quality and surge capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-265">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> Research on a universal influenza A vaccine, targeted against the external domain of the transmembrane viral <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/M2_protein" class="mw-redirect" title="M2 protein">M2 protein</a> (M2e), is being done at the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Ghent" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Ghent">University of Ghent</a> by <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Fiers" title="Walter Fiers">Walter Fiers</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xavier_Saelens" title="Xavier Saelens">Xavier Saelens</a> and their team<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-266">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-267">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-268">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> and has now successfully concluded Phase I clinical trials. There has been some research success towards a "universal flu vaccine" that produces antibodies against proteins on the viral coat which mutate less rapidly, and thus a single shot could potentially provide longer-lasting protection.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-269">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-270">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-271">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biologic_medical_product" class="mw-redirect" title="Biologic medical product">biologics</a>, therapeutic vaccines and immunobiologics are also being investigated for treatment of infection caused by viruses. Therapeutic biologics are designed to activate the immune response to virus or antigens. Typically, biologics do not target <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">metabolic pathways</a> like anti-viral drugs, but stimulate immune cells such as <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lymphocytes" class="mw-redirect" title="Lymphocytes">lymphocytes</a>, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Macrophages" class="mw-redirect" title="Macrophages">macrophages</a>, and/or <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antigen-presenting_cell" title="Antigen-presenting cell">antigen-presenting cells</a>, in an effort to drive an immune response towards a <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cytotoxic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cytotoxic">cytotoxic</a> effect against the virus. Influenza models, such as murine influenza, are convenient models to test the effects of prophylactic and therapeutic biologics. For example, <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lymphocyte_T-cell_immunomodulator" title="Lymphocyte T-cell immunomodulator">lymphocyte T-cell immunomodulator</a> inhibits viral growth in the murine model of influenza.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="Influenza#cite_note-272">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">Other animals</span></h2>
